As such, I would like to know the answer to the following questions: What weight or BMI criteria does the council have for prospective adoptive parents? Leicestershire has a BMI limit of 40 anything over this applicants would not be accepted. Anyone with a BMI limit of over 30 would be expected to demonstrate a commitment of weight loss during the assessment process and be able to provide written updates of their weight loss journey. What weight or BMI would preclude a prospective parent from adoption under the councils criteria? See answer to question 1 How many families (defined as a single adoptive parent or an adoptive couple) did the council reject for adoption because they failed to meet its weight or BMI criteria in 2019? We do not reject applicants however, we do counsel applicants in relation to the need for healthy lifestyle and longevity of life for our children. Prospective adopters are invited to return once their BMI has reduced. We do not keep records of applicants who are counselled due to weight/BMI alone, these are recorded within a general health reason. EU, all EU, European Economic Area (EEA) and Swiss citizens who want to live in the UK need to apply for a new immigration status. The EU Settlement Scheme (EUSS) has been set up to grant this status. Everyone, including children, must make an individual application. Local authorities need to identify all children and young people who are in their care, on the edge of care or have left care, who need to make an application under the EU Settlement Scheme. This includes EU, European Economic Area (EEA) and Swiss national children, or children who may be able to apply for the Settlement Scheme through a family member. Explain, justify and specify which criteria, models, grading system, professional scoring and or standards or any other system were used by the panel as a basis for a decision. Decisions relating to Education and Health Care Needs Assessment (such as decisions whether or not to proceed with an EHCNA, whether or not to issue a Plan, the contents of the Plan and the provision named in the Plan) are made with reference to the SEND (Special Educational Needs and Disability) Code of Practice (2015) and underpinning legislation/regulations, with particular reference to Chapter 9 of the CoP (Code of Practice). The SEND CoP (Special Educational Needs and Disability Code of Practice) sets out the relevant considerations (e.g. see pp 157 to 159 regarding whether to issue a Plan). There is no requirement within the CoP for the Local Authority to utilise a Panel to make these decisions. However, in Leicestershire we believe that decision making is strengthened and likely to be more consistent if relevant professionals are able to discuss the specifics of each case. In keeping with the CoP the Panel will take in to account all of the advice received as part of the assessment. In Leicestershire we currently have two Panels a Decision to Assess Panel and a SEND Panel, the latter of which considers issues regarding the nature and level of support to be offered in a Plan and (where applicable) whether or not a specialist provision is required. In terms of criteria, models, grading system, professional scoring and or standards the Panel makes decisions on a child by child basis with reference to the specific advice provided by the school and other advice givers, taking into account the parents/carers and childs views as submitted as part of the process. Regarding the naming of a school or other provision, the Local Authority has a duty to comply with parental preference providing that the provision meets three tests; in brief, that the provision can meet the childs assessed needs, that the placement would not interfere with the effective education of other children and that it is an efficient use of resources. 2. Professional roles and qualifications of any member of Leicestershire County Council and the panel involved with this process and decision makers professional bodies PIN numbers. The above functions are carried out on behalf of the Council by the SEN Assessment Service, located within the Children and Families Directorate. The Service is managed by a Service Manager who is supported by Team Managers, Senior Case Managers and Case Managers. All staff are appointed to these roles against clear person specifications that specify the skills, experience, knowledge, and qualification required for the roles. If you have framework agreements and/or block contracts for accommodation and/or support in respect to looked after children in residential care/looked after children in semi-independent placements/care leavers/uasc, please specify when each of these are due to be re-tendered. Leicestershire operates a Dynamic Purchasing System (DPS) for the provision of placement for all of the above, we do not operate any block contracts. As this is a DPS, it means that it is always open and providers can submit an application at any point in time. The DPS for residential providers was launched in February 2020 and will run for a maximum of 10 years. The DPS for semi-independent placements was launched in December 2020 and again will run for a maximum of 10 years. 3. Do organisations need to go through a pre-registration process to accommodate looked after children in residential care/looked after children in semi-independent placements/care leavers/uasc on a spot purchase basis? As Leicestershire operates a DPS, it is extremely rare we need to make a spot purchase placement however if the need arose then we would carry out a quality assurance visit with the provider before placing. This means that we would be wanting to check their policies and procedures, that they had the relevant insurances in place, ensure that all Health and Safety checks had been undertaken and seek references from other Local Authoritys that they had worked with 4. Do you have local authority run provisions for looked after children in residential care/looked after children in semi-independent placements/care leavers/uasc? What is their occupation and experience? The Short Breaks Panel is chaired by the Service Manager of the Disabled Childrens Team. The post holder is a qualified social worker and is responsible for the Disabled Childrens Service with many years experience. Other panel members include Team Managers from the Disabled Childrens Service (all social work qualified), managers from the Children and Family Wellbeing Service, a Quality Assurance Officer from SEND Services and an officer with the job role of Play, Leisure and Inclusion. All managers are qualified to degree or post graduate level or have equivalent experience. Core members of the panel: Helen Middleton, Service Manager Disabled Childrens Service, Chair Ellie Lowe, Service Manager, Children and Family Wellbeing Service Caroline Davis, Partnerships and Quality Officer, SEN and Disability Play and Leisure Inclusion Officer Team Managers from Disabled Childrens Service and the Children and Family Wellbeing Service attend on rotation. Are they formally trained in the SEND code of practice and the Children and Families Act? All members of the panel have received training on the Children and Families Act alongside an array of other relevant training. How many people sit on the panel? The panel operates with between 4 and 8 people present, depending on availability of managers. It is always chaired by a Service Manager. How do they ensure equity amongst disabled children and their families? The panel was put in place to ensure consistency in decision making, rather than decisions being made by individual managers. Leicestershire Short Breaks are considered as part of a holistic assessment of family need. This means that, as well as exploring the needs of the disabled child, consideration is given to the needs, strengths, and resources of the whole family to respond to and support that childs needs. I should be grateful if you could provide the details of these events and the evidence provided by the school to the council to substantiate what occurred. This request for information potentially engages the provisions of the Environmental Information Regulations 2004 (EIR) and the Freedom of Information Act 2000 (FOIA). Reference will be made to both information governance regimes in this response. It is not the case that Leicestershire County Council has made a positive decision to restrict public access to the playing field adjacent to Long Whatton School. To the extent that a decision has been made to restrict access to the field, it is likely to have been made by the Schools management. However, the Council is the local education authority in Leicestershire, and it has a proprietary interest in part of the land at the School Premises. Accordingly, as a means of discharging the Councils duties to provide advice and assistance (as to which see Section 16 of FOIA & Regulation 9 of EIR), the Council is content to explain its position generally regarding access to the field adjacent to the School. Leicestershire County Council supports the Schools decision to restrict access to the playing field for the following reasons: - Hygiene and Maintenance The use of the school playing field by the local community has been a concern of the school for some time. It appears reasonable to restrict access to the field if opening up public access would lead to the following nuisances: - Litter Broken Bottles Dog Mess The Council is not however in possession of documented evidence to substantiate the allegations of nuisance. Safeguarding Reasons During school operational hours the playing field cannot be made available to community users and members of the general public for Safeguarding reasons. The school and its Governors have a statutory duty under Section 175 of the Education Act 2002 to safeguard and promote the welfare of their children. Liability Reasons. Permitting visitors to access the school at all times may expose those in occupation of the land to potential civil liabilities in the event that persons visiting the premises are injured.
